<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Rice</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Staple grain with a roughly oval shape</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Rice_(disambiguation)" title="Rice (disambiguation)">Rice (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:White,_Brown,_Red_%26_Wild_rice.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/White%2C_Brown%2C_Red_%26_Wild_rice.jpg/300px-White%2C_Brown%2C_Red_%26_Wild_rice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/White%2C_Brown%2C_Red_%26_Wild_rice.jpg/450px-White%2C_Brown%2C_Red_%26_Wild_rice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/White%2C_Brown%2C_Red_%26_Wild_rice.jpg/600px-White%2C_Brown%2C_Red_%26_Wild_rice.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:White,_Brown,_Red_%26_Wild_rice.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A mixture of brown, white, and red <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indica_rice" title="Indica rice">indica rice</a>, also containing <a href="/wiki/Wild_rice" title="Wild rice">wild rice</a>, <i>Zizania</i> species</div></div></div>
<p><b>Rice</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seed</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">grass</a> <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Oryza_sativa" title="Oryza sativa">Oryza sativa</a></i> (Asian rice) or less commonly <i><a href="/wiki/Oryza_glaberrima" title="Oryza glaberrima">Oryza glaberrima</a></i> (African rice). The name <a href="/wiki/Wild_rice" title="Wild rice">wild rice</a> is usually used for species of the genera <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zizania_(genus)" title="Zizania (genus)">Zizania</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Porteresia" title="Porteresia">Porteresia</a></i>, both wild and domesticated, although the term may also be used for primitive or uncultivated varieties of <i><a href="/wiki/Oryza" title="Oryza">Oryza</a></i>.
</p><p>
As a <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereal grain</a>, domesticated rice is the most widely consumed <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple food</a> for over half of the world's <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">human population</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-feed-over-half-world_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-feed-over-half-world-1">[Liu 1]</a></sup> especially in <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>. It is the agricultural <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodity</a> with the third-highest worldwide production, after <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faostat16_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-faostat16-2">[1]</a></sup> Since sizable portions of sugarcane and maize crops are used for purposes other than human consumption, rice is the most important food crop with regard to human nutrition and caloric intake, providing more than one-fifth of the <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">calories</a> consumed worldwide by humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup> There are many varieties of rice and culinary preferences tend to vary regionally. </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rice_consumption.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="561" data-file-width="1080" decoding="async" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Rice_consumption.png/220px-Rice_consumption.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Rice_consumption.png/330px-Rice_consumption.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Rice_consumption.png/440px-Rice_consumption.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rice_consumption.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Annual per capita rice supply (2019)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oryza_sativa_of_Kadavoor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Oryza_sativa_of_Kadavoor.jpg/220px-Oryza_sativa_of_Kadavoor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Oryza_sativa_of_Kadavoor.jpg/330px-Oryza_sativa_of_Kadavoor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Oryza_sativa_of_Kadavoor.jpg/440px-Oryza_sativa_of_Kadavoor.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oryza_sativa_of_Kadavoor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Oryza_sativa" title="Oryza sativa">Oryza sativa</a></i> with small <a href="/wiki/Anemophily" title="Anemophily">wind-pollinated</a> flowers</div></div></div>
<p>The traditional method for cultivating rice is flooding the fields while, or after, setting the young seedlings. This simple method requires sound irrigation planning but reduces the growth of less robust weed and pest plants that have no submerged growth state, and deters <a href="/wiki/Vermin" title="Vermin">vermin</a>. While flooding is not mandatory for the cultivation of rice, all other methods of <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> require higher effort in <a href="/wiki/Weed_control" title="Weed control">weed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pest_control" title="Pest control">pest control</a> during growth periods and a different approach for fertilizing the soil.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brown_rice_cooked.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3260" data-file-width="4505" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Brown_rice_cooked.JPG/220px-Brown_rice_cooked.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Brown_rice_cooked.JPG/330px-Brown_rice_cooked.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Brown_rice_cooked.JPG/440px-Brown_rice_cooked.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brown_rice_cooked.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cooked brown rice from <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jumli_Marshi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3049" data-file-width="3024" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Jumli_Marshi.jpg/220px-Jumli_Marshi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Jumli_Marshi.jpg/330px-Jumli_Marshi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Jumli_Marshi.jpg/440px-Jumli_Marshi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jumli_Marshi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jumli Marshi, brown rice from <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rice_grains_(IRRI).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Rice_grains_%28IRRI%29.jpg/220px-Rice_grains_%28IRRI%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Rice_grains_%28IRRI%29.jpg/330px-Rice_grains_%28IRRI%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Rice_grains_%28IRRI%29.jpg/440px-Rice_grains_%28IRRI%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rice_grains_(IRRI).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rice can come in many shapes, colors and sizes.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Single_grain_of_rice.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1434" data-file-width="1434" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Single_grain_of_rice.png/220px-Single_grain_of_rice.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Single_grain_of_rice.png/330px-Single_grain_of_rice.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Single_grain_of_rice.png/440px-Single_grain_of_rice.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Single_grain_of_rice.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Single grain of rice under handmade microscope</div></div></div>
<p>Rice, a <a href="/wiki/Monocotyledon" title="Monocotyledon">monocot</a>, is normally grown as an <a href="/wiki/Annual_plant" title="Annual plant">annual plant</a>, although in <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropical</a> areas it can survive as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perennial" title="Perennial">perennial</a> and can produce a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ratoon" title="Ratoon">ratoon</a> crop for up to 30 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup> Rice cultivation is well-suited to countries and regions with low <a href="/wiki/Labor_theory_of_value" title="Labor theory of value">labor costs</a> and high <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rainfall</a>, as it is labor-intensive to cultivate and requires ample water. However, rice can be grown practically anywhere, even on a steep hill or <a href="/wiki/Mountain" title="Mountain">mountain</a> area with the use of water-controlling terrace systems. Although its parent species are native to Asia and certain parts of Africa, centuries of trade and exportation have made it commonplace in many cultures worldwide. Production and consumption of rice is estimated to have been responsible for 4% of global <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas emissions">greenhouse gas emissions</a> in 2010.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oryza_sativa_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-232.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="592" data-file-width="452" decoding="async" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Oryza_sativa_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-232.jpg/220px-Oryza_sativa_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-232.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Oryza_sativa_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-232.jpg/330px-Oryza_sativa_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-232.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Oryza_sativa_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-232.jpg/440px-Oryza_sativa_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-232.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oryza_sativa_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-232.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Oryza_sativa" title="Oryza sativa">Oryza sativa</a></i>, commonly known as Asian rice</div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<p>The rice plant can grow to 1–1.8 m (3–6 ft) tall, occasionally more depending on the variety and soil fertility. It has long, slender leaves 50–100 cm (20–40 in) long and <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>2–2.5 cm (<span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">3</span>⁄<span class="den">4</span></span>–1 in) broad. The small <a href="/wiki/Anemophily" title="Anemophily">wind-pollinated</a> flowers are produced in a branched arching to pendulous <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">inflorescence</a> 30–50 cm (12–20 in) long. The edible seed is a grain (<a href="/wiki/Caryopsis" title="Caryopsis">caryopsis</a>) <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>5–12 mm (<span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">3</span>⁄<span class="den">16</span></span>–<span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">15</span>⁄<span class="den">32</span></span> in) long and <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>2–3 mm (<span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">3</span>⁄<span class="den">32</span></span>–<span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">8</span></span> in) thick.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Food">Food</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cooking">Cooking</span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_rice_varieties" title="List of rice varieties">varieties of rice</a> are typically classified as long-, medium-, and short-grained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> The grains of long-grain rice (high in <a href="/wiki/Amylose" title="Amylose">amylose</a>) tend to remain intact after cooking; medium-grain rice (high in <a href="/wiki/Amylopectin" title="Amylopectin">amylopectin</a>) becomes more sticky. Medium-grain rice is used for sweet dishes, for <i><a href="/wiki/Risotto" title="Risotto">risotto</a></i> in Italy, and many rice dishes, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Arr%C3%B2s_negre" title="Arròs negre">arròs negre</a></i>, in Spain. Some varieties of long-grain rice that are high in <a href="/wiki/Amylopectin" title="Amylopectin">amylopectin</a>, known as Thai Sticky rice, are usually steamed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> A stickier short-grain rice is used for <i><a href="/wiki/Sushi" title="Sushi">sushi</a></i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kitchen2020_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kitchen2020-7">[6]</a></sup> the stickiness allows rice to hold its shape when cooked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simmons2009_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simmons2009-8">[7]</a></sup> Short-grain rice is used extensively in Japan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> including to accompany savoury dishes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlfordDuguid2003_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-AlfordDuguid2003-10">[9]</a></sup> Short-grain rice is often used for <a href="/wiki/Rice_pudding" title="Rice pudding">rice pudding</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Instant_rice" title="Instant rice">Instant rice</a> differs from <a href="/wiki/Parboiled_rice" title="Parboiled rice">parboiled rice</a> in that it is fully cooked and then dried, though there is a significant degradation in taste and texture. Rice flour and <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> often are used in <a href="/wiki/Batter_(cooking)" title="Batter (cooking)">batters</a> and breadings to increase crispiness.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Preparation">Preparation</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stages_of_rice_milling.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Stages_of_rice_milling.jpg/220px-Stages_of_rice_milling.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Stages_of_rice_milling.jpg/330px-Stages_of_rice_milling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Stages_of_rice_milling.jpg/440px-Stages_of_rice_milling.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stages_of_rice_milling.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Unmilled to milled <a href="/wiki/Japanese_rice" title="Japanese rice">Japanese rice</a>, from left to right, <a href="/wiki/Brown_rice" title="Brown rice">brown rice</a>, rice with <a href="/wiki/Cereal_germ" title="Cereal germ">germ</a>, <a href="/wiki/White_rice" title="White rice">white rice</a></div></div></div>
<p>Rinsing rice before cooking removes much of the <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a>, thereby reducing the extent to which individual grains will stick together. This yields a fluffier rice, whereas not rinsing yields a stickier and creamier result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenyon_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kenyon-11">[10]</a></sup> Rice produced in the US is usually fortified with vitamins and minerals, and rinsing will result in a loss of nutrients.
</p><p>Rice may be soaked to decrease cooking time, conserve fuel, minimize exposure to high temperature, and reduce stickiness. For some <a href="/wiki/Variety_(botany)" title="Variety (botany)">varieties</a>, soaking improves the texture of the cooked rice by increasing expansion of the grains. Rice may be soaked for 30 minutes up to several hours.
</p><p>Brown rice may be soaked in warm water for 20 hours to stimulate <a href="/wiki/Germination" title="Germination">germination</a>. This process, called <a href="/wiki/Germinated_brown_rice" title="Germinated brown rice">germinated brown rice</a> (GBR),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> activates enzymes and enhances amino acids including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma-aminobutyric_acid" title="Gamma-aminobutyric acid">gamma-aminobutyric acid</a> to improve the nutritional value of brown rice. This method is a result of research carried out for the United Nations <a href="/wiki/International_Year_of_Rice" title="International Year of Rice">International Year of Rice</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tteumul.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="466" data-file-width="699" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Tteumul.jpg/220px-Tteumul.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Tteumul.jpg/330px-Tteumul.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Tteumul.jpg/440px-Tteumul.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tteumul.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bap_(food)" title="Bap (food)">Tteumul</a>, water from the washing of rice</div></div></div>
<p>Rice is cooked by <a href="/wiki/Boiling" title="Boiling">boiling</a> or <a href="/wiki/Steaming" title="Steaming">steaming</a>, and absorbs water during cooking. With the absorption method, rice may be cooked in a volume of water equal to the volume of dry rice plus any evaporation losses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> With the rapid-boil method, rice may be cooked in a large quantity of water which is drained before serving. Rapid-boil preparation is not desirable with enriched rice, as much of the enrichment additives are lost when the water is discarded. Electric <a href="/wiki/Rice_cooker" title="Rice cooker">rice cookers</a>, popular in Asia and Latin America, simplify the process of cooking rice. Rice (or any other grain) is sometimes quickly fried in oil or fat before boiling (for example <a href="/wiki/Saffron_rice" title="Saffron rice">saffron rice</a> or <a href="/wiki/Risotto" title="Risotto">risotto</a>); this makes the cooked rice less sticky, and is a cooking style commonly called <a href="/wiki/Pilaf" title="Pilaf">pilaf</a> in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> or <a href="/wiki/Biryani" title="Biryani">biryani</a> in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dishes">Dishes</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rice_Animation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="643" data-file-width="1048" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Rice_Animation.gif/220px-Rice_Animation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Rice_Animation.gif/330px-Rice_Animation.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Rice_Animation.gif/440px-Rice_Animation.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rice_Animation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>-<a href="/wiki/Rice#Processing" title="Rice">Rice processing</a>-<br/> A: Rice with <a href="/wiki/Chaff" title="Chaff">chaff</a><br/>B: <a href="/wiki/Brown_rice" title="Brown rice">Brown rice</a><br/>C: Rice with <a href="/wiki/Cereal_germ" title="Cereal germ">germ</a><br/>D: <a href="/wiki/White_rice" title="White rice">White rice</a> with <a href="/wiki/Bran" title="Bran">bran</a> <a href="/wiki/Crop_residue" title="Crop residue">residue</a><br/>E: Musenmai (Japanese: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musenmai" title="Musenmai">無洗米</a>), "Polished and ready to boil rice", literally, non-wash rice<br/>(1): <a href="/wiki/Chaff" title="Chaff">Chaff</a><br/>(2): <a href="/wiki/Bran" title="Bran">Bran</a><br/>(3): Bran <a href="/wiki/Crop_residue" title="Crop residue">residue</a><br/>(4): <a href="/wiki/Cereal_germ" title="Cereal germ">Cereal germ</a><br/>(5): <a href="/wiki/Endosperm" title="Endosperm">Endosperm</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_rice_dishes" title="List of rice dishes">List of rice dishes</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Arab_cuisine" title="Arab cuisine">Arab cuisine</a>, rice is an ingredient of many soups and dishes with fish, poultry, and other types of meat. It is used to stuff vegetables or is wrapped in grape leaves (<a href="/wiki/Dolma" title="Dolma">dolma</a>). When combined with milk, sugar, and honey, it is used to make desserts. In some regions, such as <a href="/wiki/Tabaristan" title="Tabaristan">Tabaristan</a>, bread is made using rice flour. Rice may be made into <a href="/wiki/Congee" title="Congee">congee</a> (also called rice porridge or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rice_gruel" title="Rice gruel">rice gruel</a>) by adding more water than usual, so that the cooked rice is saturated with water, usually to the point that it disintegrates. Rice porridge is commonly eaten as a breakfast food, and is a traditional food for the sick.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition">Nutrition</span></h3>
<p>Rice is the <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple food</a> of over half the world's population. It is the predominant dietary energy source for 17 countries in Asia and the Pacific, 9 countries in North and South America and 8 countries in Africa. Rice provides 20% of the world's dietary energy supply, while wheat supplies 19% and maize (corn) 5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cooked unenriched long-grain white rice is composed of 68% water, 28% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrates</a>, 3% <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, and 1% <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> (table). A <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>100-gram (<span class="frac" role="math">3<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">2</span></span>-ounce) reference serving of it provides 540 kilojoules (130 kilocalories) of <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food energy</a> and contains no <a href="/wiki/Micronutrient" title="Micronutrient">micronutrients</a> in significant amounts, with all less than 10% of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daily_Value" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a> (DV) (table). Cooked short-grain white rice provides the same food energy and contains moderate amounts of <a href="/wiki/B_vitamins" title="B vitamins">B vitamins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> (10–17% DV) per 100-gram serving (table).
</p><p>A detailed analysis of nutrient content of rice suggests that the nutrition value of rice varies based on a number of factors. It depends on the strain of rice, such as <a href="/wiki/White_rice" title="White rice">white</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brown_rice" title="Brown rice">brown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_rice" title="Red rice">red</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Black_rice" title="Black rice">black</a> (or purple) varieties having different prevalence across world regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who2018_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-who2018-15">[14]</a></sup> It also depends on nutrient quality of the soil rice is grown in, whether and how the rice is polished or processed, the manner it is enriched, and how it is prepared before consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2018 <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) guideline showed that <a href="/wiki/Food_fortification" title="Food fortification">fortification</a> of rice to reduce <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> may involve different micronutrient strategies, including <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> only, iron with <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A" title="Vitamin A">vitamin A</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folic_acid" title="Folic acid">folic acid</a>, or iron with other <a href="/wiki/B_vitamins" title="B vitamins">B-complex vitamins</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thiamin" title="Thiamin">thiamin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niacin_(nutrient)" title="Niacin (nutrient)">niacin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B6" title="Vitamin B6">vitamin B6</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pantothenic_acid" title="Pantothenic acid">pantothenic acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who2018_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-who2018-15">[14]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Systematic_review" title="Systematic review">systematic review</a> of <a href="/wiki/Clinical_research" title="Clinical research">clinical research</a> on the efficacy of rice fortification showed the strategy had the main effect of reducing the risk of <a href="/wiki/Iron_deficiency" title="Iron deficiency">iron deficiency</a> by 35% and increasing blood levels of <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who2018_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-who2018-15">[14]</a></sup> The guideline established a major recommendation: "Fortification of rice with iron is recommended as a public health strategy to improve the iron status of populations, in settings where rice is a staple food."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who2018_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-who2018-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rice grown experimentally under elevated <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> levels, similar to those predicted for the year 2100 <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">as a result of human activity</a>, had less iron, zinc, and protein, as well as lower levels of thiamin, <a href="/wiki/Riboflavin" title="Riboflavin">riboflavin</a>, folic acid, and pantothenic acid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup>
The following table shows the nutrient content of rice and other major staple foods in a raw form on a <a href="/wiki/Dry_matter#Dry_matter_basis" title="Dry matter">dry weight basis</a> to account for their different water contents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arsenic_concerns">Arsenic concerns</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arsenic_poisoning" title="Arsenic poisoning">Arsenic poisoning</a></div>
<p>As arsenic occurs in soil, water, and air, the United States <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> (FDA) monitors the levels of arsenic in foods, particularly in rice products used commonly for infant food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-ars_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-fda-ars-20">[19]</a></sup> While growing, rice plants tend to absorb arsenic more readily than other food crops, requiring expanded testing by the FDA for possible arsenic-related risks associated with rice consumption in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-ars_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-fda-ars-20">[19]</a></sup> In April 2016, the FDA proposed a limit of 100 parts per billion (ppb) for inorganic arsenic in infant <a href="/wiki/Rice_cereal" title="Rice cereal">rice cereal</a> and other foods to minimize exposure of infants to arsenic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-ars_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-fda-ars-20">[19]</a></sup> For water contamination by arsenic, the United States <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="Environmental Protection Agency">Environmental Protection Agency</a> has set a lower standard of 10 ppb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-epa-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Arsenic is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_IARC_Group_1_carcinogens" title="List of IARC Group 1 carcinogens">Group 1 carcinogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-ars_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-fda-ars-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EFSA2009_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-EFSA2009-22">[21]</a></sup> The amount of arsenic in rice varies widely with the greatest concentration in brown rice and rice grown on land formerly used to grow cotton, such as in <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CR1112_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-CR1112-23">[22]</a></sup> White rice grown in Arkansas, Louisiana, Missouri, and Texas, which account collectively for 76 percent of American-produced rice, had higher levels of arsenic than other regions of the world studied, possibly because of past use of arsenic-based pesticides to control cotton weevils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> Jasmine rice from Thailand and Basmati rice from Pakistan and India contain the least arsenic among rice varieties in one study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cpotera_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-cpotera-25">[24]</a></sup> China has set a limit of 150 ppb for arsenic in rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacillus_cereus"><i>Bacillus cereus</i></span></h3>
<p>Cooked rice can contain <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_cereus" title="Bacillus cereus">Bacillus cereus</a></i> spores, which produce an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emetic" title="Emetic">emetic</a> toxin when left at 4–60 °C (39–140 °F). When storing cooked rice for use the next day, rapid cooling is advised to reduce the risk of toxin production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> One of the <a href="/wiki/Enterotoxin" title="Enterotoxin">enterotoxins</a> produced by <i>Bacillus cereus</i> is heat-resistant; reheating contaminated rice kills the bacteria, but does not destroy the toxin already present.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rice-growing_environments">Rice-growing environments</span></h2>
<p>Rice growth and production are affected by: the environment, soil properties, biotic conditions, and cultural practices. Environmental factors include rainfall and water, temperature, photoperiod, solar radiation and, in some instances, tropical storms. Soil factors refer to soil type and their position in uplands or lowlands. Biotic factors deal with weeds, insects, diseases, and crop varieties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rice can be grown in different environments, depending upon water availability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> Generally, rice does not thrive in a waterlogged area, yet it can survive and grow herein<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> and it can survive flooding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><b>Lowland, rainfed</b>, which is drought prone, favors medium depth; waterlogged, submergence, and flood prone</li>
<li><b> Lowland, irrigated</b>, grown in both the wet season and the dry season</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_water_rice" title="Deep water rice">Deep water</a></b> or floating rice</li>
<li><b>Coastal wetland</b></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Upland_rice" title="Upland rice">Upland rice</a></b> (also known as <b>hill rice</b> or <b>Ghaiya rice</b>) is well known for its drought tolerance<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Domestication"></span>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_cultivation">History of cultivation</span></h2>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/History_of_rice_cultivation" title="History of rice cultivation">History of rice cultivation</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_rice_cultivation&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
The <a href="/wiki/History_of_rice_cultivation" title="History of rice cultivation">history of rice cultivation</a> is an interdisciplinary subject that studies archaeological and documentary evidence to explain how rice was first domesticated and cultivated by humans, the spread of cultivation to different regions of the planet, and the technological changes that have impacted cultivation over time. The current scientific consensus, based on archaeological and linguistic evidence, is that <i><a href="/wiki/Oryza_sativa" title="Oryza sativa">Oryza sativa</a></i> rice was first domesticated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze River</a> basin in China 13,500 to 8,200 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_of_rice_cultivation_Normile_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_rice_cultivation_Normile-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_of_rice_cultivation_Vaughanetal2008_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_rice_cultivation_Vaughanetal2008-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_of_rice_cultivation_harris_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_rice_cultivation_harris-35">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_of_rice_cultivation_Zhang2012_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_rice_cultivation_Zhang2012-36">[35]</a></sup><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KITLV_40091_-_Kassian_C%C3%A9phas_-_Relief_of_the_hidden_base_of_Borobudur_-_1890-1891.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1889" data-file-width="4210" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/KITLV_40091_-_Kassian_C%C3%A9phas_-_Relief_of_the_hidden_base_of_Borobudur_-_1890-1891.jpg/300px-KITLV_40091_-_Kassian_C%C3%A9phas_-_Relief_of_the_hidden_base_of_Borobudur_-_1890-1891.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/KITLV_40091_-_Kassian_C%C3%A9phas_-_Relief_of_the_hidden_base_of_Borobudur_-_1890-1891.jpg/450px-KITLV_40091_-_Kassian_C%C3%A9phas_-_Relief_of_the_hidden_base_of_Borobudur_-_1890-1891.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/KITLV_40091_-_Kassian_C%C3%A9phas_-_Relief_of_the_hidden_base_of_Borobudur_-_1890-1891.jpg/600px-KITLV_40091_-_Kassian_C%C3%A9phas_-_Relief_of_the_hidden_base_of_Borobudur_-_1890-1891.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KITLV_40091_-_Kassian_C%C3%A9phas_-_Relief_of_the_hidden_base_of_Borobudur_-_1890-1891.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bas-relief at <a href="/wiki/Karmawibhangga_Museum" title="Karmawibhangga Museum">Karmawibhanga Museum in Central Java</a> of 9th century <a href="/wiki/Borobudur" title="Borobudur">Borobudur</a> describe <a href="/wiki/Rice_barn" title="Rice barn">rice barn</a> and rice plants being infested by mouse pestilence. <a href="/wiki/Rice_production_in_Indonesia" title="Rice production in Indonesia">Rice farming</a> has a long history in Indonesia.</div></div></div>From that first cultivation, migration and trade spread rice around the world - first to much of east Asia, and then further abroad, and eventually to the Americas as part of the <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">Columbian exchange</a>.  The now less common <i><a href="/wiki/Oryza_glaberrima" title="Oryza glaberrima">Oryza glaberrima</a></i> rice was independently domesticated in Africa 3,000 to 3,500 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_of_rice_cultivation_:0_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_rice_cultivation_:0-37">[36]</a></sup> Other <a href="/wiki/Wild_rice" title="Wild rice">wild rices</a> have also been cultivated in different geographies, such as in the Americas.Since its spread, rice has become a global staple crop important to food security and food cultures around the world. Local varieties of  <i><a href="/wiki/Oryza_sativa" title="Oryza sativa">Oryza sativa</a></i> have resulted in over <a href="/wiki/List_of_rice_cultivars" title="List of rice cultivars">40,000 cultivars of various types</a>. More recent changes in agricultural practices and breeding methods as part of the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a> and other transfers of agricultural technologies has led to increased production in recent decades, with emergence of new types such as <a href="/wiki/Golden_rice" title="Golden rice">golden rice</a>, which was genetically engineered to contain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta-Carotene" title="Beta-Carotene">beta carotene</a>.</div></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_rice_production" title="List of countries by rice production">List of countries by rice production</a></div><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RiceYield.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/RiceYield.png/220px-RiceYield.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/RiceYield.png/330px-RiceYield.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/RiceYield.png/440px-RiceYield.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RiceYield.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Worldwide rice production</div></div></div><p>In 2020, world production of paddy rice was 756.7 million metric tons (834.1 million short tons)s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> led by China and India with a combined 52% of this total.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faostat16_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-faostat16-2">[1]</a></sup> Other major producers were <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>. The five major producers accounted for 72% of total production, while the top fifteen producers accounted for 91% of total world production in 2017 (see table on right). Developing countries account for 95% of the total production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAORice1_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAORice1-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Production_of_rice_(2019).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="308" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Production_of_rice_%282019%29.svg/300px-Production_of_rice_%282019%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Production_of_rice_%282019%29.svg/450px-Production_of_rice_%282019%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Production_of_rice_%282019%29.svg/600px-Production_of_rice_%282019%29.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Production_of_rice_(2019).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Production of rice (2019)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Rice is a major food staple and a mainstay for the rural population and their food security. It is mainly cultivated by small farmers in holdings of less than one <a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">hectare</a>. Rice is also a wage commodity for workers in the cash crop or non-agricultural sectors. Rice is vital for the nutrition of much of the population in Asia, as well as in Latin America and the Caribbean and in Africa; it is central to the food security of over half the world population.
</p><p>Many rice grain producing countries have significant losses post-harvest at the farm and because of poor roads, inadequate storage technologies, inefficient supply chains and farmer's inability to bring the produce into retail markets dominated by small shopkeepers. A World Bank – FAO study claims 8% to 26% of rice is lost in developing nations, on average, every year, because of post-harvest problems and poor infrastructure. Some sources claim the post-harvest losses exceed 40%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAORice1_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAORice1-40">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> Not only do these losses reduce food security in the world, the study claims that farmers in developing countries such as China, India and others lose approximately US$89 billion of income in preventable post-harvest farm losses, poor transport, the lack of proper storage and retail. One study claims that if these post-harvest grain losses could be eliminated with better infrastructure and retail network, in India alone enough food would be saved every year to feed 70 to 100 million people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Processing">Processing</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NP_India_burning_48_(6315309342).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/NP_India_burning_48_%286315309342%29.jpg/220px-NP_India_burning_48_%286315309342%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/NP_India_burning_48_%286315309342%29.jpg/330px-NP_India_burning_48_%286315309342%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/NP_India_burning_48_%286315309342%29.jpg/440px-NP_India_burning_48_%286315309342%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NP_India_burning_48_(6315309342).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burning of rice residues after harvest, to quickly prepare the land for <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a> planting, around <a href="/wiki/Sangrur" title="Sangrur">Sangrur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_India" title="Punjab, India">Punjab, India</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The seeds of the rice plant are first milled using a <a href="/wiki/Rice_huller" title="Rice huller">rice huller</a> to remove the <a href="/wiki/Chaff" title="Chaff">chaff</a> (the outer husks of the grain) (see: <a href="/wiki/Rice_hulls" title="Rice hulls">rice hulls</a>). At this point in the process, the product is called <a href="/wiki/Brown_rice" title="Brown rice">brown rice</a>. The milling may be continued, removing the <a href="/wiki/Bran" title="Bran">bran</a>, <i>i.e.</i>, the rest of the husk and the <a href="/wiki/Cereal_germ" title="Cereal germ">germ</a>, thereby creating <a href="/wiki/White_rice" title="White rice">white rice</a>. White rice, which keeps longer, lacks some important nutrients; moreover, in a limited diet which does not supplement the rice, brown rice helps to prevent the <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beriberi" title="Beriberi">beriberi</a>.
</p><p>Either by hand or in a <a href="/wiki/Rice_polisher" title="Rice polisher">rice polisher</a>, white rice may be buffed with glucose or <a href="/wiki/Talc" title="Talc">talc</a> powder (often called polished rice, though this term may also refer to white rice in general), <a href="/wiki/Parboiled_rice" title="Parboiled rice">parboiled</a>, or processed into flour. White rice may also be enriched by adding nutrients, especially those lost during the milling process. While the cheapest method of enriching involves adding a powdered blend of nutrients that will easily wash off (in the United States, rice which has been so treated requires a label warning against rinsing), more sophisticated methods apply nutrients directly to the grain, coating the grain with a water-insoluble substance which is resistant to washing.
</p><p>In some countries, a popular form, <a href="/wiki/Parboiled_rice" title="Parboiled rice">parboiled rice</a> (also known as <b>converted rice</b> and <b>easy-cook rice</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup>) is subjected to a steaming or <a href="/wiki/Parboiling" title="Parboiling">parboiling</a> process while still a brown rice grain. The parboil process causes a gelatinisation of the starch in the grains. The grains become less brittle, and the color of the milled grain changes from white to yellow. The rice is then dried, and can then be milled as usual or used as brown rice. Milled parboiled rice is nutritionally superior to standard milled rice, because the process causes nutrients from the outer husk (especially <a href="/wiki/Thiamine" title="Thiamine">thiamine</a>) to move into the <a href="/wiki/Endosperm" title="Endosperm">endosperm</a>, so that less is subsequently lost when the husk is polished off during milling. Parboiled rice has an additional benefit in that it does not stick to the pan during cooking, as happens when cooking regular white rice. This type of rice is eaten in parts of India and countries of West Africa are also accustomed to consuming parboiled rice.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rice_bran" title="Rice bran">Rice bran</a>, called <i>nuka</i> in Japan, is a valuable commodity in Asia and is used for many daily needs. It is a moist, oily inner layer which is heated to produce oil. It is also used as a pickling bed in making <a href="/wiki/Nukazuke" title="Nukazuke">rice bran pickles</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Takuan" title="Takuan">takuan</a></i>.
</p><p>Raw rice may be ground into flour for many uses, including making many kinds of beverages, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Amazake" title="Amazake">amazake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horchata" title="Horchata">horchata</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Rice_milk" title="Rice milk">rice milk</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rice_wine" title="Rice wine">rice wine</a>. Rice does not contain <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a>, so is suitable for people on a <a href="/wiki/Gluten-free_diet" title="Gluten-free diet">gluten-free diet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PenaginiDilillo2013_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-PenaginiDilillo2013-45">[44]</a></sup> Rice can be made into various types of <a href="/wiki/Noodle" title="Noodle">noodles</a>. Raw, wild, or brown rice may also be consumed by <a href="/wiki/Raw_foodism" title="Raw foodism">raw-foodist</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fruitarians" title="Fruitarians">fruitarians</a> if soaked and <a href="/wiki/Sprouting" title="Sprouting">sprouted</a> (usually a week to 30 days – gaba rice).
</p><p>Processed rice seeds must be boiled or steamed before eating. Boiled rice may be further fried in <a href="/wiki/Cooking_oil" title="Cooking oil">cooking oil</a> or butter (known as <a href="/wiki/Fried_rice" title="Fried rice">fried rice</a>), or beaten in a tub to make <i><a href="/wiki/Mochi" title="Mochi">mochi</a></i>.
</p><p>Rice is a good source of protein and a staple food in many parts of the world, but it is not a <a href="/wiki/Complete_protein" title="Complete protein">complete protein</a>: it does not contain all of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acids" title="Essential amino acids">essential amino acids</a> in sufficient amounts for good health, and should be combined with other sources of protein, such as nuts, seeds, beans, fish, or meat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Protein_content_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Protein_content-46">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rice, like other cereal <a href="/wiki/Caryopsis" title="Caryopsis">grains</a>, can be <a href="/wiki/Puffed_rice" title="Puffed rice">puffed (or popped)</a>. This process takes advantage of the grains' water content and typically involves heating grains in a special chamber. Further puffing is sometimes accomplished by processing puffed pellets in a low-<a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> chamber. The <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas_law" title="Ideal gas law">ideal gas law</a> means either lowering the local pressure or raising the water temperature results in an increase in <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volume</a> prior to water <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a>, resulting in a puffy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Texture_(food)" title="Texture (food)">texture</a>. Bulk raw rice density is about 0.9 g/cm<sup>3</sup>. It decreases to less than one-tenth that when puffed.
</p>
<h3><span id="Harvesting.2C_drying_and_milling"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Harvesting,_drying_and_milling">Harvesting, drying and milling</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rice-combine-harvester,_Katori-city,_Japan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rice-combine-harvester%2C_Katori-city%2C_Japan.jpg/220px-Rice-combine-harvester%2C_Katori-city%2C_Japan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rice-combine-harvester%2C_Katori-city%2C_Japan.jpg/330px-Rice-combine-harvester%2C_Katori-city%2C_Japan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rice-combine-harvester%2C_Katori-city%2C_Japan.jpg/440px-Rice-combine-harvester%2C_Katori-city%2C_Japan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rice-combine-harvester,_Katori-city,_Japan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rice combine harvester <a href="/wiki/Katori,_Chiba" title="Katori, Chiba">Katori-city</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chiba_Prefecture" title="Chiba Prefecture">Chiba Prefecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1316" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg/220px-Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg/330px-Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg/440px-Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>After the harvest, rice straw is gathered in the traditional way from small paddy fields in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mae_Wang_District" title="Mae Wang District">Mae Wang District</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chiang_Mai_Province" title="Chiang Mai Province">Chiang Mai Province</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Paddy_field" title="Paddy field">Paddy field</a></div>
<p>Unmilled rice, known as "paddy" (Indonesia and Malaysia: padi; Philippines, palay), is usually harvested when the grains have a moisture content of around 25%. In most Asian countries, where rice is almost entirely the product of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smallholder" title="Smallholder">smallholder</a> agriculture, harvesting is carried out manually, although there is a growing interest in mechanical harvesting. Harvesting can be carried out by the farmers themselves, but is also frequently done by seasonal labor groups. Harvesting is followed by <a href="/wiki/Threshing" title="Threshing">threshing</a>, either immediately or within a day or two. Again, much threshing is still carried out by hand but there is an increasing use of mechanical threshers. Subsequently, paddy needs to be dried to bring down the moisture content to no more than 20% for milling.
</p><p>A familiar sight in several Asian countries is paddy laid out to dry along roads. However, in most countries the bulk of drying of marketed paddy takes place in mills, with village-level drying being used for paddy to be consumed by farm families. Mills either sun dry or use mechanical driers or both. Drying has to be carried out quickly to avoid the formation of molds. Mills range from simple <a href="/wiki/Rice_huller" title="Rice huller">hullers</a>, with a throughput of a couple of tonnes a day, that simply remove the outer husk, to enormous operations that can process 4 thousand metric tons (4.4 thousand short tons) a day and produce highly polished rice. A good mill can achieve a paddy-to-rice conversion rate of up to 72% but smaller, inefficient mills often struggle to achieve 60%. These smaller mills often do not buy paddy and sell rice but only service farmers who want to mill their paddy for their own consumption.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">Distribution</span></h3>
<p>Because of the importance of rice to human nutrition and food security in Asia, the domestic rice markets tend to be subject to considerable state involvement. While the private sector plays a leading role in most countries, agencies such as <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_Bureau_of_Logistics" title="Indonesian Bureau of Logistics">BULOG</a> in Indonesia, the <a href="/wiki/National_Food_Authority_(Philippines)" title="National Food Authority (Philippines)">NFA</a> in the Philippines, VINAFOOD in Vietnam and the <a href="/wiki/Food_Corporation_of_India" title="Food Corporation of India">Food Corporation of India</a> are all heavily involved in purchasing of paddy from farmers or rice from mills and in distributing rice to poorer people. BULOG and NFA monopolise rice imports into their countries while VINAFOOD controls all exports from Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nellu.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Nellu.JPG/220px-Nellu.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Nellu.JPG/330px-Nellu.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Nellu.JPG/440px-Nellu.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nellu.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Drying rice in <a href="/wiki/Peravoor" title="Peravoor">Peravoor</a>, India</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade">Trade</span></h3>
<p>World trade figures are very different from those for production, as less than 8% of rice produced is traded internationally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cendrowski2013_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-cendrowski2013-48">[47]</a></sup> In economic terms, the global rice trade was a small fraction of 1% of world mercantile trade. Many countries consider rice as a strategic food staple, and various governments subject its trade to a wide range of controls and interventions.
</p><p>Developing countries are the main players in the world rice trade, accounting for 83% of exports and 85% of imports. While there are numerous importers of rice, the exporters of rice are limited. Just five countries—Thailand, Vietnam, China, the United States and India—in decreasing order of exported quantities, accounted for about three-quarters of world rice exports in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAORice1_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-FAORice1-40">[39]</a></sup> However, this ranking has been rapidly changing in recent years. In 2010, the three largest exporters of rice, in decreasing order of quantity exported were Thailand, Vietnam and India. By 2012, India became the largest exporter of rice with a 100% increase in its exports on year-to-year basis, and Thailand slipped to third position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> Together, Thailand, Vietnam and India accounted for nearly 70% of the world rice exports.
</p><p>The primary variety exported by Thailand and Vietnam were <a href="/wiki/Jasmine_rice" title="Jasmine rice">Jasmine rice</a>, while exports from India included aromatic <a href="/wiki/Basmati" title="Basmati">Basmati</a> variety. China, an exporter of rice in early 2000s, was a net importer of rice in 2010 and will become the largest net importer, surpassing Nigeria, in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cendrowski2013_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-cendrowski2013-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> According to a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">USDA</a> report, the world's largest exporters of rice in 2012 were India (9.75 million metric tons (10.75 million short tons)), Vietnam (7 million metric tons (7.7 million short tons)), Thailand (6.5 million metric tons (7.2 million short tons)), Pakistan (3.75 million metric tons (4.13 million short tons)) and the United States (3.5 million metric tons (3.9 million short tons)).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Major importers usually include Nigeria, Indonesia, Bangladesh, Saudi Arabia, Iran, Iraq, Malaysia, the Philippines, Brazil and some African and <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> countries. In common with other West African countries, Nigeria is actively promoting domestic production. However, its very heavy import duties (110%) open it to smuggling from neighboring countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> Parboiled rice is particularly popular in Nigeria. Although China and India are the two largest producers of rice in the world, both countries consume the majority of the rice produced domestically, leaving little to be traded internationally.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Yield_records">Yield records</span></h3>
<p>The average world yield for rice was 4.3 metric tons per hectare (1.9 short tons per acre), in 2010. Australian rice farms were the most productive in 2010, with a nationwide average of 10.8 metric tons per hectare (4.8 short tons per acre).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Yuan_Longping" title="Yuan Longping">Yuan Longping</a> of China National Hybrid Rice Research and Development Center set a world record for rice yield in 2010 at 19 metric tons per hectare (8.5 short tons per acre) on a demonstration plot. In 2011, this record was reportedly surpassed by an Indian farmer, Sumant Kumar, with 22.4 metric tons per hectare (10.0 short tons per acre) in <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, although this claim has been disputed by both Yuan and India's <a href="/wiki/Central_Rice_Research_Institute" title="Central Rice Research Institute">Central Rice Research Institute</a>. These efforts employed newly developed rice breeds and <a href="/wiki/System_of_Rice_Intensification" title="System of Rice Intensification">System of Rice Intensification</a> (SRI), a recent innovation in rice farming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Price">Price</span></h2>

src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2021</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In late 2007 to May 2008, the price of grains rose greatly due to droughts in major producing countries (particularly Australia), increased use of grains for animal feed and US subsidies for bio-fuel production. Although there was no shortage of rice on world markets this general upward trend in grain prices led to panic buying by consumers, government rice export bans (in particular, by Vietnam and India) and inflated import orders by the Philippines marketing board, the National Food Authority. This caused significant rises in rice prices. In late April 2008, prices hit 24 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_cent" title="US cent">US cents</a> a <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">pound</a>, twice the price of seven months earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cyclone_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-cyclone-59">[58]</a></sup> Over the period of 2007 to 2013, the Chinese government has substantially increased the price it pays domestic farmers for their rice, rising to <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>500 per metric ton by 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cendrowski2013_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-cendrowski2013-48">[47]</a></sup> The 2013 price of rice originating from other southeast Asian countries was a comparably low <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>350 per metric ton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cendrowski2013_48-3"><a href="#cite_note-cendrowski2013-48">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>On April 30, 2008, Thailand announced plans for the creation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Rice_Exporting_Countries" title="Organisation of Rice Exporting Countries">Organisation of Rice Exporting Countries</a> (OREC) with the intention that this should develop into a price-fixing <a href="/wiki/Cartel" title="Cartel">cartel</a> for rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> However, as of mid-2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Rice&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> little progress had been made to achieve this.
</p>
