<p>As of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Rice&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, world food consumption of rice was 565.6 million metric tons (623.5 million short tons) of paddy equivalent (377,283 metric tons (415,883 short tons) of milled equivalent), while the largest consumers were China consuming 162.4 million metric tons (179.0 million short tons) of paddy equivalent (28.7% of world consumption) and India consuming 130.4 million metric tons (143.7 million short tons) of paddy equivalent (23.1% of world consumption).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-con_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-con-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 1961 and 2002, per capita consumption of rice increased by 40% worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> A paper from the Korean Society of Crop Science anticipated that consumption would increase to 590 million tons by 2040, and that consumption would decline in Asia and increase in other parts of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rice is the most important crop in Asia. In Cambodia, for example, 90% of the total agricultural area is used for rice production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> Per capita, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> ranks as the country with the highest rice consumption, followed by <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>U.S. rice consumption has risen sharply over the past 25 years, fueled in part by commercial applications such as beer production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> Almost one in five adult Americans now report eating at least half a serving of white or brown rice per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impacts">Environmental impacts</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NP_Rice_Emissions18_(5687953086).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/NP_Rice_Emissions18_%285687953086%29.jpg/220px-NP_Rice_Emissions18_%285687953086%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/NP_Rice_Emissions18_%285687953086%29.jpg/330px-NP_Rice_Emissions18_%285687953086%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/NP_Rice_Emissions18_%285687953086%29.jpg/440px-NP_Rice_Emissions18_%285687953086%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NP_Rice_Emissions18_(5687953086).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Work by the <a href="/wiki/International_Center_for_Tropical_Agriculture" title="International Center for Tropical Agriculture">International Center for Tropical Agriculture</a> to measure the greenhouse gas emissions of rice production.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span></h3>
<p>The worldwide production of rice accounts for more <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas emissions">greenhouse gas emissions</a> (GHG) in total than that of any other plant food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-69">[68]</a></sup> It was estimated in 2021 to be responsible for 30% of agricultural <a href="/wiki/Methane_emissions" title="Methane emissions">methane emissions</a> and 11% of agricultural <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> Methane release is caused by long-term flooding of rice fields, inhibiting the soil from absorbing atmospheric oxygen, a process causing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaerobic_fermentation" title="Anaerobic fermentation">anaerobic fermentation</a> of organic matter in the soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heinz_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-heinz-71">[70]</a></sup> A 2021 study estimated that rice contributed 2 billion tonnes of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">anthropogenic greenhouse gases</a> in 2010,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-69">[68]</a></sup> of the 47 billion total.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> The study added up GHG emissions from the entire lifecycle, including production, transportation, and consumption, and compared the global totals of different foods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> The total for rice was half the total for beef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-:7-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2010 study found that, as a result of rising temperatures and decreasing solar radiation during the later years of the 20th century, the rice yield growth rate has decreased in many parts of Asia, compared to what would have been observed had the temperature and solar radiation trends not occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> The yield growth rate had fallen 10–20% at some locations. The study was based on records from 227 farms in Thailand, Vietnam, Nepal, India, China, Bangladesh, and Pakistan. The mechanism of this falling yield was not clear, but might involve increased respiration during warm nights, which expends energy without being able to photosynthesize. More detailed analysis of rice yields by the <a href="/wiki/International_Rice_Research_Institute" title="International Rice Research Institute">International Rice Research Institute</a> forecast 20% reduction in yields in Asia per degree Celsius of temperature rise. Rice becomes sterile if exposed to temperatures above 35 degrees for more than one hour during flowering and consequently produces no grain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_usage">Water usage</span></h3>
<p>Rice requires slightly more water to produce than other grains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> Rice production uses almost a third of Earth's fresh water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> Water outflows from rice fields through <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpiration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a>, seepage, and <a href="/wiki/Percolation" title="Percolation">percolation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-80">[79]</a></sup> It is estimated that it takes about 2,500 litres (660 US gal) of water need to be supplied to account for all of these outflows and produce 1 kilogram (2 lb 3 oz) of rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Pests"></span><span class="anchor" id="Diseases"></span>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pests_and_diseases">Pests and diseases</span></h2>
<p>Rice pests are any <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbe" title="Microbe">microbes</a> with the potential to reduce the yield or value of the rice crop (or of rice seeds).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> Rice pests include weeds, <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a>, insects, nematode, rodents, and birds. A variety of factors can contribute to pest outbreaks, including climatic factors, improper irrigation, the overuse of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insecticides" title="Insecticides">insecticides</a> and high rates of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fertilizer" title="Nitrogen fertilizer">nitrogen fertilizer</a> application.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> Weather conditions also contribute to pest outbreaks. For example, rice <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gall_midge" title="Gall midge">gall midge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spodoptera_mauritia" title="Spodoptera mauritia">army worm</a> outbreaks tend to follow periods of high rainfall early in the wet season, while <a href="/wiki/Thrips" title="Thrips">thrips</a> outbreaks are associated with drought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_pests">Animal pests</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Insects">Insects</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_rice_grasshopper_(Oxya_chinensis).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2288" data-file-width="3432" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Chinese_rice_grasshopper_%28Oxya_chinensis%29.jpg/220px-Chinese_rice_grasshopper_%28Oxya_chinensis%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Chinese_rice_grasshopper_%28Oxya_chinensis%29.jpg/330px-Chinese_rice_grasshopper_%28Oxya_chinensis%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Chinese_rice_grasshopper_%28Oxya_chinensis%29.jpg/440px-Chinese_rice_grasshopper_%28Oxya_chinensis%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_rice_grasshopper_(Oxya_chinensis).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chinese rice grasshopper<br/>(<i>Oxya chinensis</i>)<br/><a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a></div></div></div>
<p>Major rice insect pests include: the <a href="/wiki/Brown_planthopper" title="Brown planthopper">brown planthopper</a> (BPH),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> several species of <a href="/wiki/Stemborer" title="Stemborer">stemborers</a>—including those in the genera <i><a href="/wiki/Scirpophaga" title="Scirpophaga">Scirpophaga</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Chilo_suppressalis" title="Chilo suppressalis">Chilo</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RKB_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-RKB-85">[84]</a></sup> the rice <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gall_midge" title="Gall midge">gall midge</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> several species of <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Rice_bug_(disambiguation)" title="Rice bug (disambiguation)">rice bugs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-j1_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-j1-87">[86]</a></sup> notably in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Leptocorisa" title="Leptocorisa">Leptocorisa</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> defoliators such as the rice: <a href="/wiki/Cnaphalocrocis_medinalis" title="Cnaphalocrocis medinalis">leafroller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dicladispa_armigera" title="Dicladispa armigera">hispa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxya" title="Oxya">grasshoppers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Fall_armyworm" title="Fall armyworm">fall army worm</a>, a species of Lepidoptera, also targets and causes damage to rice crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rice_weevil" title="Rice weevil">Rice weevils</a> attack stored produce.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nematodes">Nematodes</span></h4>
<p>Several <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematode</a> species infect rice crops, causing diseases such as Ufra (<i><a href="/wiki/Ditylenchus_dipsaci" title="Ditylenchus dipsaci">Ditylenchus dipsaci</a></i>), White tip disease (<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Aphelenchoide_bessei&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Aphelenchoide bessei (page does not exist)">Aphelenchoide bessei</a></i>), and root knot disease (<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Meloidogyne_graminicola&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Meloidogyne graminicola (page does not exist)">Meloidogyne graminicola</a></i>). Some nematode species such as <i><a href="/wiki/Pratylenchus" title="Pratylenchus">Pratylenchus</a></i> spp. are most dangerous in upland rice of all parts of the world. Rice root nematode (<i><a href="/wiki/Hirschmanniella_oryzae" title="Hirschmanniella oryzae">Hirschmanniella oryzae</a></i>) is a migratory endoparasite which on higher inoculum levels will lead to complete destruction of a rice crop. Beyond being obligate parasites, they also decrease the vigor of plants and increase the plants' susceptibility to other pests and diseases.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_pests">Other pests</span></h4>
<p>These include the apple snail <i><a href="/wiki/Pomacea_canaliculata" title="Pomacea canaliculata">Pomacea canaliculata</a></i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panicle_rice_mite" title="Panicle rice mite">panicle rice mite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rat" title="Rat">rats</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> and the weed <i><a href="/wiki/Echinochloa" title="Echinochloa">Echinochloa</a> crusgali</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diseases">Diseases</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_rice_diseases" title="List of rice diseases">List of rice diseases</a></div>
<p>Rice blast, caused by the fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Magnaporthe_grisea" title="Magnaporthe grisea">Magnaporthe grisea</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> is the most significant disease affecting rice cultivation. It and <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_leaf_streak" title="Bacterial leaf streak">bacterial leaf streak</a> (caused by <i><a href="/wiki/Xanthomonas_oryzae_pv._oryzae" title="Xanthomonas oryzae pv. oryzae">Xanthomonas oryzae pv. oryzae</a></i>) are perennially the two worst rice diseases worldwide, and such is their importance - and the importance of rice - that they are both among the top 10 diseases of plants in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mg-Xoo-worst_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mg-Xoo-worst-94">[Liu 2]</a></sup> Other major fungal and bacterial rice diseases include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sheath_blight" title="Sheath blight">sheath blight</a> (caused by <i><a href="/wiki/Rhizoctonia_solani" title="Rhizoctonia solani">Rhizoctonia solani</a></i>), false smut (<i><a href="/wiki/Ustilaginoidea_virens" title="Ustilaginoidea virens">Ustilaginoidea virens</a></i>), bacterial panicle blight (<i><a href="/wiki/Burkholderia_glumae" title="Burkholderia glumae">Burkholderia glumae</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-other-major-dis_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-other-major-dis-95">[Liu 3]</a></sup> sheath rot (<i><a href="/wiki/Sarocladium_oryzae" title="Sarocladium oryzae">Sarocladium oryzae</a></i>), and <i><a href="/wiki/Bakanae" title="Bakanae">bakanae</a></i> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fusarium_fujikuroi" title="Fusarium fujikuroi">Fusarium fujikuroi</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Table-1_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Table-1-96">[Liu 4]</a></sup> Viral diseases exist, such as <a href="/wiki/Rice_ragged_stunt_virus" title="Rice ragged stunt virus">rice ragged stunt</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" title="Vector (epidemiology)">vector</a>: BPH), and <a href="/wiki/Tungrovirus" title="Tungrovirus">tungro</a> (vector: <i><a href="/wiki/Nephotettix" title="Nephotettix">Nephotettix</a></i> spp).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[93]</a></sup> Many viral diseases, especially those <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" title="Vector (epidemiology)">vectored</a> by <a href="/wiki/Planthopper" title="Planthopper">planthoppers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leafhopper" title="Leafhopper">leafhoppers</a>, are major causes of losses across the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hibino-1996_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hibino-1996-98">[94]</a></sup> There is also an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascomycete" title="Ascomycete">ascomycete</a> fungus, <i><a href="/wiki/Cochliobolus_miyabeanus" title="Cochliobolus miyabeanus">Cochliobolus miyabeanus</a></i>, that causes brown spot disease in rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Table-1_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-Table-1-96">[Liu 4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Integrated_pest_management">Integrated pest management</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Integrated_pest_management" title="Integrated pest management">Integrated pest management</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Crop_protection" title="Crop protection">Crop protection</a> scientists are trying to develop rice pest management techniques which are <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">sustainable</a>. In other words, to manage crop pests in such a manner that future crop production is not threatened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[97]</a></sup> Sustainable pest management is based on four principles: biodiversity, host plant resistance (HPR),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LSU-AgCenter-varieties-2021_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-LSU-AgCenter-varieties-2021-102">[98]</a></sup> landscape ecology, and hierarchies in a landscape—from biological to social.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[99]</a></sup> At present, rice pest management includes cultural techniques, pest-resistant rice varieties,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LSU-AgCenter-varieties-2021_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-LSU-AgCenter-varieties-2021-102">[98]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pesticides" title="Pesticides">pesticides</a> (which include <a href="/wiki/Insecticide" title="Insecticide">insecticide</a>). Increasingly, there is evidence that farmers' pesticide applications are often unnecessary, and even facilitate pest outbreaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[103]</a></sup> By reducing the populations of natural enemies of rice pests,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[104]</a></sup> misuse of insecticides can actually lead to pest outbreaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[105]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Rice_Research_Institute" title="International Rice Research Institute">International Rice Research Institute</a> (IRRI) demonstrated in 1993 that an 87.5% reduction in pesticide use can lead to an overall drop in pest numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[106]</a></sup> IRRI also conducted two campaigns in 1994 and 2003, respectively, which discouraged insecticide misuse and smarter pest management in Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rice plants produce their own chemical defenses to protect themselves from pest attacks. Some synthetic chemicals, such as the herbicide 2,4-D, cause the plant to increase the production of certain defensive chemicals and thereby increase the plant's resistance to some types of pests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[109]</a></sup> Conversely, other chemicals, such as the insecticide <a href="/wiki/Imidacloprid" title="Imidacloprid">imidacloprid</a>, can induce changes in the gene expression of the rice that cause the plant to become more susceptible to attacks by certain types of pests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[110]</a></sup> 5-<a href="/wiki/Alkylresorcinol" title="Alkylresorcinol">Alkylresorcinols</a> are chemicals that can also be found in rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>Botanicals, so-called "natural pesticides", are used by some farmers in an attempt to control rice pests. Botanicals include extracts of leaves, or a mulch of the leaves themselves. Some upland rice farmers in Cambodia spread chopped leaves of the bitter bush (<i><a href="/wiki/Chromolaena_odorata" title="Chromolaena odorata">Chromolaena odorata</a></i>) over the surface of fields after planting. This practice probably helps the soil retain moisture and thereby facilitates seed germination. Farmers also claim the leaves are a natural fertilizer and helps suppress weed and insect infestations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG/220px-Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG/330px-Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG/440px-Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chloroxylon is used for pest management in organic rice cultivation in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chhattisgarh,_India" title="Chhattisgarh, India">Chhattisgarh, India</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Among rice cultivars, there are differences in the responses to, and recovery from, pest damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-j1_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-j1-87">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LSU-AgCenter-varieties-2021_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-LSU-AgCenter-varieties-2021-102">[98]</a></sup> Many rice varieties have been selected for resistance to insect pests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LSU-AgCenter-varieties-2021_102-3"><a href="#cite_note-LSU-AgCenter-varieties-2021-102">[98]</a></sup> Therefore, particular cultivars are recommended for areas prone to certain pest problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LSU-AgCenter-varieties-2021_102-4"><a href="#cite_note-LSU-AgCenter-varieties-2021-102">[98]</a></sup> The genetically based ability of a rice variety to withstand pest attacks is called resistance. Three main types of plant resistance to pests are recognized as nonpreference, antibiosis, and tolerance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-j2_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-j2-120">[116]</a></sup> Nonpreference (or antixenosis) describes host plants which insects prefer to avoid; antibiosis is where insect survival is reduced after the ingestion of host tissue; and tolerance is the capacity of a plant to produce high yield or retain high quality despite <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Home_stored_product_entomology" title="Home stored product entomology">insect infestation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Over time, the use of pest-resistant rice varieties selects for pests that are able to overcome these mechanisms of resistance. When a rice variety is no longer able to resist pest infestations, resistance is said to have broken down. Rice varieties that can be widely grown for many years in the presence of pests and retain their ability to withstand the pests are said to have durable resistance. Mutants of popular rice varieties are regularly screened by plant breeders to discover new sources of durable resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-j2_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-j2-120">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parasitic_weeds">Parasitic weeds</span></h3>
<p>Rice is parasitized by the weed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eudicot" title="Eudicot">eudicot</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Striga_hermonthica" title="Striga hermonthica">Striga hermonthica</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[119]</a></sup> which is of local importance for this crop.
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Ecotypes"></span><span class="anchor" id="Ecotype"></span><span class="anchor" id="Cultivars"></span><span class="anchor" id="Cultivar"></span>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecotypes_and_cultivars">Ecotypes and cultivars</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_rice_cultivars" title="List of rice cultivars">List of rice cultivars</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rice_diversity.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3600" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Rice_diversity.jpg/220px-Rice_diversity.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Rice_diversity.jpg/330px-Rice_diversity.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Rice_diversity.jpg/440px-Rice_diversity.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rice_diversity.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rice seed collection from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IRRI" title="IRRI">IRRI</a></div></div></div>
<p>While most rice is bred for crop quality and productivity, there are varieties selected for characteristics such as texture, smell, and firmness. There are four major categories of rice worldwide: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indica_rice" title="Indica rice">indica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japonica_rice" title="Japonica rice">japonica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aromatic_rice" title="Aromatic rice">aromatic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glutinous_rice" title="Glutinous rice">glutinous</a>. The different varieties of rice are not considered interchangeable, either in food preparation or agriculture, so as a result, each major variety is a completely separate market from other varieties. It is common for one variety of rice to rise in price while another one drops in price.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rice cultivars also fall into groups according to environmental conditions, season of planting, and season of harvest, called ecotypes. Some major groups are the Japan-type (grown in Japan), "buly" and "tjereh" types (Indonesia); <i>sali</i> (or <i>aman</i>—main winter crop), <i>ahu</i> (also <i>aush</i> or <i>ghariya</i>, summer), and <i>boro</i> (spring) (Bengal and Assam).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Banglapedia_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Banglapedia-126">[122]</a></sup> Cultivars exist that are adapted to deep flooding, and these are generally called "floating rice".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>The largest collection of rice cultivars is at the <a href="/wiki/International_Rice_Research_Institute" title="International Rice Research Institute">International Rice Research Institute</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[124]</a></sup> in the Philippines, with over 100,000 rice accessions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[125]</a></sup> held in the International Rice Genebank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[126]</a></sup> Rice <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a> are often classified by their grain shapes and texture. For example, Thai <a href="/wiki/Jasmine_rice" title="Jasmine rice">Jasmine rice</a> is long-grain and relatively less sticky, as some long-grain rice contains less <a href="/wiki/Amylopectin" title="Amylopectin">amylopectin</a> than short-grain cultivars. Chinese restaurants often serve long-grain as plain unseasoned steamed rice though short-grain rice is common as well. Japanese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mochigome" title="Mochigome">mochi rice</a> and Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sticky_rice" title="Sticky rice">sticky rice</a> are short-grain. Chinese people use sticky rice which is properly known as "glutinous rice" (note: glutinous refer to the glue-like characteristic of rice; does not refer to "gluten") to make <a href="/wiki/Zongzi" title="Zongzi">zongzi</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Japanese_rice" title="Japanese rice">Japanese table rice</a> is a sticky, short-grain rice. Japanese <a href="/wiki/Sake" title="Sake">sake</a> rice is another kind as well.
</p><p>Indian rice cultivars include long-grained and aromatic <a href="/wiki/Basmati" title="Basmati">Basmati</a> (ਬਾਸਮਤੀ) (grown in the North), long and medium-grained <a href="/wiki/Patna_rice" title="Patna rice">Patna rice</a>, and in South India (<a href="/wiki/Andhra_Pradesh" title="Andhra Pradesh">Andhra Pradesh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a>) short-grained <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sona_Masuri" title="Sona Masuri">Sona Masuri</a> (also called as Bangaru theegalu). In the state of Tamil Nadu, the most prized cultivar is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ponni_Rice" title="Ponni Rice"><i>ponni</i></a> which is primarily grown in the delta regions of the <a href="/wiki/Kaveri" title="Kaveri">Kaveri</a> River. <a href="/wiki/Kaveri" title="Kaveri">Kaveri</a> is also referred to as ponni in the South and the name reflects the geographic region where it is grown. In the Western Indian state of <a href="/wiki/Maharashtra" title="Maharashtra">Maharashtra</a>, a short grain variety called <a href="/wiki/Ambemohar" title="Ambemohar">Ambemohar</a> is very popular. This rice has a characteristic fragrance of Mango blossom.
</p><p>Aromatic rices have definite aromas and flavors; the most noted cultivars are Thai fragrant rice, Basmati, Patna rice, Vietnamese fragrant rice, and a <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrid</a> cultivar from America, sold under the trade name Texmati. Both Basmati and Texmati have a mild <a href="/wiki/Popcorn" title="Popcorn">popcorn</a>-like aroma and flavor. In Indonesia, there are also <i>red</i> and <i>black</i> cultivars.
</p><p>High-yield cultivars of rice suitable for cultivation in Africa and other dry <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystems" title="Ecosystems">ecosystems</a>, called the <a href="/wiki/New_Rice_for_Africa" title="New Rice for Africa">new rice for Africa</a> (NERICA) cultivars, have been developed. It is hoped that their cultivation will improve <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a> in West Africa.
</p><p>Draft <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genomes" title="Genomes">genomes</a> for the two most common rice cultivars, <i>indica</i> and <i>japonica</i>, were published in April 2002. Rice was chosen as a <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model organism</a> for the biology of grasses because of its relatively small genome (~430 mega<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Base_pairs" title="Base pairs">base pairs</a>). Rice was the first crop with a complete genome sequence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>On December 16, 2002, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_General_Assembly" title="UN General Assembly">UN General Assembly</a> declared the year 2004 the International Year of Rice. The declaration was sponsored by more than 40 countries.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Varietal_development" title="Varietal development">Varietal development</a> has ceremonial and historical significance for some cultures (see <a href="#Culture">§ Culture</a> below). The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_Thailand" title="King of Thailand">Thai kings</a> have patronised rice breeding since at least the reign of <a href="/wiki/Chulalongkorn" title="Chulalongkorn">Chulalongkorn</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thai-Min-Ag-hist_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thai-Min-Ag-hist-132">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FFTC-AP-rice-breeding_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FFTC-AP-rice-breeding-133">[129]</a></sup> and his great-great-grandson <a href="/wiki/Vajiralongkorn" title="Vajiralongkorn">Vajiralongkorn</a> released five particular rice varieties to celebrate <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Vajiralongkorn" title="Coronation of Vajiralongkorn">his coronation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vajiralongkorn-coron_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vajiralongkorn-coron-134">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biotechnology">Biotechnology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High-yielding_varieties">High-yielding varieties</span></h3>
<p>The high-yielding varieties are a group of crops created intentionally during the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a> to increase global food production. This project enabled labor markets in Asia to shift away from agriculture, and into industrial sectors. The first "Rice Car", IR8 was produced in 1966 at the <a href="/wiki/International_Rice_Research_Institute" title="International Rice Research Institute">International Rice Research Institute</a> which is based in the Philippines at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_the_Philippines" title="University of the Philippines">University of the Philippines</a>' Los Baños site. IR8 was created through a cross between an Indonesian variety named "Peta" and a Chinese variety named "Dee Geo Woo Gen."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ir8_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-ir8-135">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scientists have identified and cloned many genes involved in the <a href="/wiki/Gibberellin" title="Gibberellin">gibberellin</a> signaling pathway, including GAI1 (<a href="/wiki/Gibberellin" title="Gibberellin">Gibberellin</a> Insensitive) and SLR1 (Slender Rice).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[132]</a></sup> Disruption of <a href="/wiki/Gibberellin" title="Gibberellin">gibberellin</a> signaling can lead to significantly reduced stem growth leading to a dwarf phenotype. Photosynthetic investment in the stem is reduced dramatically as the shorter plants are inherently more stable mechanically. Assimilates become redirected to grain production, amplifying in particular the effect of chemical fertilizers on commercial yield. In the presence of nitrogen fertilizers, and intensive crop management, these varieties increase their yield two to three times.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_potential">Future potential</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Update plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="Ambox current red Americas.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>. The reason given is: gene editing? low ghg emissions?.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2021</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>As the UN Millennium Development project seeks to spread global economic development to Africa, the "Green Revolution" is cited as the model for economic development. With the intent of replicating the successful Asian boom in agronomic productivity, groups like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_Institute" title="Earth Institute">Earth Institute</a> are doing research on African agricultural systems, hoping to increase productivity. An important way this can happen is the production of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Rices_for_Africa" title="New Rices for Africa">New Rices for Africa</a>" (NERICA). These rices, selected to tolerate the low input and harsh growing conditions of African agriculture, are produced by the African Rice Center, and billed as technology "from Africa, for Africa". The NERICA have appeared in <i>The New York Times</i> (October 10, 2007) and <i>International Herald Tribune</i> (October 9, 2007), trumpeted as miracle crops that will dramatically increase rice yield in Africa and enable an economic resurgence. Ongoing research in China to develop <a href="/wiki/Perennial_rice" title="Perennial rice">perennial rice</a> could result in enhanced sustainability and <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Golden_rice">Golden rice</span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Golden_rice" title="Golden rice">Golden rice</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Golden_rice&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Golden_Rice.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2391" data-file-width="3600" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Golden_Rice.jpg/220px-Golden_Rice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Golden_Rice.jpg/330px-Golden_Rice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Golden_Rice.jpg/440px-Golden_Rice.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Golden_Rice.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Golden rice (right) compared to white rice (left)</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with "gold" varieties of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_rice" title="African rice">African rice</a>, which have golden-coloured seedcoats, not golden endosperm; or with <a href="/wiki/Yellow_rice" title="Yellow rice">Yellow rice</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Golden_rice" title="Golden rice">Golden Rice</a> is a variety of rice (<i><a href="/wiki/Oryza_sativa" title="Oryza sativa">Oryza sativa</a></i>) produced through <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a> to <a href="/wiki/Biosynthesis" title="Biosynthesis">biosynthesize</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta-carotene" title="Beta-carotene">beta-carotene</a>, a precursor of <a href="/wiki/Retinol" title="Retinol">vitamin A</a>, in the edible parts of the rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golden_rice_ye2000_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Golden_rice_ye2000-138">[134]</a></sup> It is intended to produce a fortified food to be grown and consumed in areas with a <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A_deficiency" title="Vitamin A deficiency">shortage of dietary vitamin A</a>. Vitamin A deficiency causes xerophthalmia, a range of eye conditions from night blindness to more severe clinical outcomes such as keratomalacia and corneal scars, and permanent blindness. It also increases risk of mortality from measles and diarrhea in children. In 2013, the prevalence of deficiency was the highest in sub-Saharan Africa (48%; 25–75), and South Asia (44%; 13–79).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golden_rice_Stevens2015_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Golden_rice_Stevens2015-139">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
Although golden rice has met significant opposition from <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmental</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anti-globalization_movement" title="Anti-globalization movement">anti-globalisation</a> activists, more than 100 Nobel laureates in 2016 encouraged use of genetically modified golden rice which can produce up to 23 times as much beta-carotene as the original golden rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golden_rice_paine2005_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Golden_rice_paine2005-140">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golden_rice_Tang_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Golden_rice_Tang-141">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golden_rice_Datta_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Golden_rice_Datta-142">[138]</a></sup></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expression_of_human_proteins">Expression of human proteins</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ventria_Bioscience" title="Ventria Bioscience">Ventria Bioscience</a> has <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetically_modified" title="Genetically modified">genetically modified</a> rice to <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">express</a> <a href="/wiki/Lactoferrin" title="Lactoferrin">lactoferrin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lysozyme" title="Lysozyme">lysozyme</a> which are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proteins" title="Proteins">proteins</a> usually found in <a href="/wiki/Breast_milk" title="Breast milk">breast milk</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Human_serum_albumin" title="Human serum albumin">human serum albumin</a>, These proteins have <a href="/wiki/Antiviral_protein" title="Antiviral protein">antiviral</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibacterial" title="Antibacterial">antibacterial</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antifungal_protein" title="Antifungal protein">antifungal</a> effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rice containing these added proteins can be used as a component in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oral_rehydration_solution" title="Oral rehydration solution">oral rehydration solutions</a> which are used to treat <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrheal</a> diseases, thereby shortening their duration and reducing recurrence. Such supplements may also help reverse <a href="/wiki/Anemia" title="Anemia">anemia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flood-tolerant_rice">Flood-tolerant rice</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Deepwater_rice" title="Deepwater rice">Deepwater rice</a></div>
<p>Due to the varying levels that water can reach in regions of cultivation, <a href="/wiki/Deepwater_rice" title="Deepwater rice">flood tolerant varieties</a> have long been developed and used. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flooding" title="Flooding">Flooding</a> is an issue that many rice growers face, especially in South and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_East_Asia" title="South East Asia">South East Asia</a> where flooding annually affects 20 million hectares (49 million acres).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-145">[141]</a></sup> Standard rice varieties cannot withstand stagnant flooding of more than about a week,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[142]</a></sup> mainly as it disallows the plant access to necessary requirements such as sunlight and essential gas exchanges, inevitably leading to plants being unable to recover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-145">[141]</a></sup>
In the past, this has led to massive losses in yields, such as in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, where in 2006, rice crops worth $65 million were lost to flooding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-147">[143]</a></sup> Recently developed cultivars seek to improve flood tolerance.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Drought-tolerant_rice">Drought-tolerant rice</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">Drought</a> represents a significant environmental stress for rice production, with 19–23 million hectares (47–57 million acres) of rainfed rice production in South and South East Asia often at risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-irri.org_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-irri.org-148">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-149">[145]</a></sup> Under drought conditions, without sufficient water to afford them the ability to obtain the required levels of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nutrients" title="Nutrients">nutrients</a> from the soil, conventional commercial rice varieties can be severely affected—for example, yield losses as high as 40% have affected some parts of India, with resulting losses of around US$800 million annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-150">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Rice_Research_Institute" title="International Rice Research Institute">International Rice Research Institute</a> conducts research into developing drought-tolerant rice varieties, including the varieties 5411 and Sookha dhan, currently being employed by farmers in the Philippines and Nepal respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-149">[145]</a></sup> In addition, in 2013 the Japanese National Institute for Agrobiological Sciences led a team which successfully inserted the DEEPER ROOTING 1 (DRO1) gene, from the Philippine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upland_and_lowland_(freshwater_ecology)" title="Upland and lowland (freshwater ecology)">upland</a> rice variety Kinandang Patong, into the popular commercial rice variety IR64, giving rise to a far deeper root system in the resulting plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-150">[146]</a></sup> This facilitates an improved ability for the rice plant to derive its required nutrients in times of drought via accessing deeper layers of <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a>, a feature demonstrated by trials which saw the IR64 + DRO1 rice yields drop by 10% under moderate drought conditions, compared to 60% for the unmodified IR64 variety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_150-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-150">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salt-tolerant_rice">Salt-tolerant rice</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Crop_tolerance_to_seawater" title="Crop tolerance to seawater">Crop tolerance to seawater</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Soil_salinity" title="Soil salinity">Soil salinity</a> poses a major threat to rice crop productivity, particularly along low-lying coastal areas during the dry season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-irri.org_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-irri.org-148">[144]</a></sup> For example, roughly 1 million hectares (2.5 million acres) of the coastal areas of <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> are affected by saline soils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[148]</a></sup> These high concentrations of salt can severely affect rice plants' normal <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a>, especially during early stages of growth, and as such farmers are often forced to abandon these otherwise potentially usable areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-153">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>Progress has been made, however, in developing rice varieties capable of tolerating such conditions; the hybrid created from the cross between the commercial rice variety IR56 and the wild rice species <i>Oryza coarctata</i> is one example.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-155">[151]</a></sup> <i>O. coarctata</i> is capable of successful growth in soils with double the limit of salinity of normal varieties, but lacks the ability to produce edible rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-155">[151]</a></sup> Developed by the <a href="/wiki/International_Rice_Research_Institute" title="International Rice Research Institute">International Rice Research Institute</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrid</a> variety can utilise specialised leaf glands that allow for the removal of salt into the atmosphere. It was initially produced from one successful <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a> out of 34,000 crosses between the two species; this was then <a href="/wiki/Backcrossing" title="Backcrossing">backcrossed</a> to IR56 with the aim of preserving the genes responsible for salt tolerance that were inherited from <i>O. coarctata</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-153">[149]</a></sup> Extensive trials are planned prior to the new variety being available to farmers by approximately 2017–18.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_153-2"><a href="#cite_note-:4-153">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rice_egypt.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="236" data-file-width="423" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Rice_egypt.png/250px-Rice_egypt.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Rice_egypt.png/375px-Rice_egypt.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5e/Rice_egypt.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rice_egypt.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The irrigated rice (paddy) crop in Egypt has a salt tolerance of ECe=5.5 dS/m beyond which the yield declines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[152]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>When the problem of <a href="/wiki/Soil_salinity" title="Soil salinity">soil salinity</a> arises it will be opportune to select salt tolerant varieties (IRRI<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[153]</a></sup> or to resort to <a href="/wiki/Soil_salinity_control" title="Soil salinity control">soil salinity control</a>.
</p><p>Soil salinity is often measured as the electric conductivity (EC) of the extract of a saturated soil paste (ECe). The EC units are usually expressed in decisiemens per metre or dS/m. The critical ECe value of 5.5 dS/m in the figure, obtained from measurements in farmers' fields, indicates that the rice crop is slightly salt sensitive.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment-friendly_rice">Environment-friendly rice</span></h3>
<p>Producing rice in <a href="/wiki/Paddy_field" title="Paddy field">paddies</a> is harmful for the environment due to the release of methane by <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogenic bacteria</a>. These bacteria live in the anaerobic waterlogged soil, and live off nutrients released by rice roots. Researchers have recently reported in <i>Nature</i> that putting the <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a> gene SUSIBA2 into rice creates a shift in biomass production from root to shoot (above ground tissue becomes larger, while below ground tissue is reduced), decreasing the methanogen population, and resulting in a reduction of methane emissions of up to 97%. Apart from this environmental benefit, the modification also increases the amount of rice grains by 43%, which makes it a useful tool in feeding a growing world population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Meiosis_and_DNA_repair">Meiosis and DNA repair</span></h2>
<p>Rice is used as a model organism for investigating the molecular mechanisms of meiosis and <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair" title="DNA repair">DNA repair</a> in higher plants. Meiosis is a key stage of the sexual cycle in which diploid cells in the <a href="/wiki/Ovule" title="Ovule">ovule</a> (female structure) and the <a href="/wiki/Stamen" title="Stamen">anther</a> (male structure) produce haploid cells that develop further into gametophytes and <a href="/wiki/Gamete#Plants" title="Gamete">gametes</a>. So far, 28 meiotic genes of rice have been characterized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[156]</a></sup> Studies of rice gene OsRAD51C showed that this gene is necessary for <a href="/wiki/Homologous_recombination" title="Homologous recombination">homologous recombinational repair</a> of DNA, particularly the accurate repair of DNA double-strand breaks during meiosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[157]</a></sup> Rice gene OsDMC1 was found to be essential for pairing of homologous chromosomes during meiosis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[158]</a></sup> and rice gene OsMRE11 was found to be required for both synapsis of homologous chromosomes and repair of double-strand breaks during meiosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[159]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Culture"></span>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_roles_of_rice">Cultural roles of rice</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Beeld_van_Dewi_Sri_de_rijstgodin_TMnr_60016918.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="513" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Beeld_van_Dewi_Sri_de_rijstgodin_TMnr_60016918.jpg/220px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Beeld_van_Dewi_Sri_de_rijstgodin_TMnr_60016918.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Beeld_van_Dewi_Sri_de_rijstgodin_TMnr_60016918.jpg/330px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Beeld_van_Dewi_Sri_de_rijstgodin_TMnr_60016918.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Beeld_van_Dewi_Sri_de_rijstgodin_TMnr_60016918.jpg/440px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Beeld_van_Dewi_Sri_de_rijstgodin_TMnr_60016918.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Beeld_van_Dewi_Sri_de_rijstgodin_TMnr_60016918.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ancient statue of <a href="/wiki/Dewi_Sri" title="Dewi Sri">Dewi Sri</a> from Java (c. 9th century)</div></div></div>
<p>Rice plays an important role in certain religions and popular beliefs. In many cultures relatives will scatter rice during or towards the end of a <a href="/wiki/Wedding" title="Wedding">wedding</a> ceremony in front of the bride and groom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Pounded_rice_ritual" title="Pounded rice ritual">pounded rice ritual</a> is conducted during weddings in <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>. The bride gives a leafplate full of pounded rice to the groom after he requests it politely from her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> rice wine, popularly known as <i><a href="/wiki/Tapuy" title="Tapuy">tapuy</a></i>, is used for important occasions such as weddings, rice harvesting ceremonies and other celebrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Dewi_Sri" title="Dewi Sri">Dewi Sri</a> is the traditional rice goddess of the <a href="/wiki/Javanese_people" title="Javanese people">Javanese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sundanese_people" title="Sundanese people">Sundanese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Balinese_people" title="Balinese people">Balinese people</a> in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>. Most rituals involving Dewi Sri are associated with the mythical origin attributed to the rice plant, the staple food of the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[164]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, a similar rice deity is known as <i><a href="/wiki/Phosop" title="Phosop">Phosop</a></i>; she is a deity more related to ancient local folklore than a goddess of a structured, mainstream religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[165]</a></sup> The same female rice deity is known as <i>Po Ino Nogar</i> in <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a> and as <i>Nang Khosop</i> in <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>. Ritual offerings are made during the different stages of rice production to propitiate the Rice Goddess in the corresponding cultures.
</p><p>A 2014 study of <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> communities found that a history of farming rice makes cultures more psychologically interdependent, whereas a history of farming wheat makes cultures more independent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Royal_Ploughing_Ceremony" title="Royal Ploughing Ceremony">Royal Ploughing Ceremony</a> is held in certain Asian countries to mark the beginning of the rice planting season. It is still honored in the kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xinhua-Camb-plough-2017_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xinhua-Camb-plough-2017-171">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Khmer-Times_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Khmer-Times-172">[168]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Star-Thai-coron-plough_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Star-Thai-coron-plough-173">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thai-plough-cancel-2021_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thai-plough-cancel-2021-174">[170]</a></sup> The 2,600-year-old tradition – begun by <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Auddhodana" title="Śuddhodana">Śuddhodana</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kapilavastu_(ancient_city)" title="Kapilavastu (ancient city)">Kapilavastu</a> – was revived in the republic of <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> in 2017 after a lapse of a few years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sen-2019_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sen-2019-175">[171]</a></sup>
</p><p>Thai king <a href="/wiki/Vajiralongkorn" title="Vajiralongkorn">Vajiralongkorn</a> released five particular rice varieties to celebrate <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Vajiralongkorn" title="Coronation of Vajiralongkorn">his coronation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vajiralongkorn-coron_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vajiralongkorn-coron-134">[130]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-feed-over-half-world-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-feed-over-half-world_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Abstract, "Rice feeds more than half the world's population."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Mg-Xoo-worst-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Mg-Xoo-worst_94-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">p.<span class="nowrap"> </span>214, "Rice blast (caused by the fungal pathogen <i>Magnaporthe oryzae</i>) and bacterial blight (caused by the bacterial pathogen <i>Xanthomonas oryzae</i> pv. <i>oryzae</i>) are the most devastating rice diseases (119) and are among the 10 most important fungal and bacterial diseases in plants (32, 95). Owing to their scientific and economic importance, both pathosystems have been the focus of concentrated study over the past two decades, and they are now advanced molecular models for plant fungal and bacterial diseases."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-other-major-dis-95"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-other-major-dis_95-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">p.<span class="nowrap"> </span>214, "...other diseases, including rice sheath blight (caused by the fungal pathogen <i>Rhizoctonia solani</i>), false smut (caused by the fungal pathogen <i>Ustilaginoidea virens</i>), bacterial leaf streak (caused by <i>X. oryzae</i> pv. <i>oryzicola</i>), bacterial panicle blight (<i>Burkholderia glumae</i>), are emerging globally as important rice diseases (53, 72, 180) (Figure 1)."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Table-1-96"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Table-1_96-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Table-1_96-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">p.<span class="nowrap"> </span>214, Table 1: Important fungal and bacterial diseases in rice.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<ul><li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFLiuLiuTriplettLeach2014">Liu W, Liu J, Triplett L, Leach JE, Wang GL (August 4, 2014). "Novel insights into rice innate immunity against bacterial and fungal pathogens". <i>Annual Review of Phytopathology</i>. <a href="/wiki/Annual_Reviews_(publisher)" title="Annual Reviews (publisher)">Annual Reviews</a>. <b>52</b> (1): 213–241. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1146%2Fannurev-phyto-102313-045926" rel="nofollow">10.1146/annurev-phyto-102313-045926</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/24906128" rel="nofollow">24906128</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:9244874" rel="nofollow">9244874</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Annual+Review+of+Phytopathology&amp;rft.atitle=Novel+insights+into+rice+innate+immunity+against+bacterial+and+fungal+pathogens&amp;rft.volume=52&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=213-241&amp;rft.date=2014-08-04&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A9244874%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F24906128&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1146%2Fannurev-phyto-102313-045926&amp;rft.aulast=Liu&amp;rft.aufirst=W&amp;rft.au=Liu%2C+J&amp;rft.au=Triplett%2C+L&amp;rft.au=Leach%2C+JE&amp;rft.au=Wang%2C+GL&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARice"></span></li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFDeb2019">Deb D (October 2019). "Restoring Rice Biodiversity". <i><a href="/wiki/Scientific_American" title="Scientific American">Scientific American</a></i>. <b>321</b> (4): 54–61. <q>India originally possessed some 110,000 <a href="/wiki/Landrace" title="Landrace">landraces</a> of rice with diverse and valuable properties. These include enrichment in vital nutrients and the ability to withstand flood, drought, salinity or pest infestations. The <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a> covered fields with a few high-yielding varieties, so that roughly 90 percent of the landraces vanished from farmers' collections. High-yielding varieties require expensive inputs. They perform abysmally on marginal farms or in adverse environmental conditions, forcing poor farmers into debt.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Scientific+American&amp;rft.atitle=Restoring+Rice+Biodiversity&amp;rft.volume=321&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.pages=54-61&amp;rft.date=2019-10&amp;rft.aulast=Deb&amp;rft.aufirst=D&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARice"></span></li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFSingh2018">Singh BN (2018). <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20180314175208/http://www.studiumpress.in/global-rice-cultivation-cultivars.html" rel="nofollow"><i>Global Rice Cultivation &amp; Cultivars</i></a>. New Delhi: Studium Press Llc. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-62699-107-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-62699-107-1"><bdi>978-1-62699-107-1</bdi></a>. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://www.studiumpress.in/global-rice-cultivation-cultivars.html" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on March 14, 2018<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">March 14,</span> 2018</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Global+Rice+Cultivation+%26+Cultivars&amp;rft.place=New+Delhi&amp;rft.pub=Studium+Press+Llc&amp;rft.date=2018&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-62699-107-1&amp;rft.aulast=Singh&amp;rft.aufirst=BN&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.studiumpress.in%2Fglobal-rice-cultivation-cultivars.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARice"></span></li></ul>
